His act had merely stripped a veneer, revealing the crude, hydraulic pistons beneath. The public story would be adjusted, a clumsier lie told, the dissonance explained away as bureaucratic friction. The world, desperate for order, would accept the new story. It was easier.

His war of evidence was lost.

But the woman in the cable chamber, with her tailored coat and her eyes of polished flint, had revealed the true battlefield. It was not in documents, but in dogma. Not in proof, but in premise. Their power was a narrative one, a totalizing story that explained the world and their rightful place as its authors. He had introduced an irrational variable. A man who fought not for a counter-truth, but for the sanctity of the lie itself. A forger who refused to forge for the regime. He was nonsense. And nonsense, in a system built on flawless, rationalized control, was a toxin.

He needed to become a carrier.

The lantern’s meager light seemed to pulse with the slow beat of his heart. He looked at his hands, pale in the gloom. They were the instruments of his old art. They knew the pressure required to mimic the tremor of a 16th-century cartographer’s pen, the exact viscosity of ink to feign age, the gentle persuasion needed to marry new pigment to ancient fiber. They were creators of plausible fictions.

A new fiction was required. Not a document to disprove theirs, but a story to unravel the very fabric of theirs. A story so inherently, compellingly flawed that it would act as an acid, eating through the certainty of their narrative.

He thought of the city above him, sleeping under its blanket of sanctioned history. Of the tourists who would tomorrow file through Sint-Baafs to see the Adoration of the Mystic Lamb, its panels a testament to a divine order. Of the bureaucrats in their steel-and-glass atrium, marshaling data flows, their reality a clean, digital parable. His fiction had to bridge those worlds. It had to be a ghost in the cathedral and a virus in the server.

He moved to the far wall of the cistern, where a niche was shielded by a sheet of mildewed canvas. He pulled it back. Here was his archive of the real—the raw, un-narrated substrata of Ghent. Not the grand portraits, but the marginalia. A sheaf of 1920s sewer blueprints, showing tunnels even he had not yet walked. A ledger from a defunct wool guild, detailing bribes paid to medieval city magistrates. A bundle of love letters from a German occupation officer to a Belgian resistance cellist, their passion a messy, human counterpoint to the grand narrative of war. A collection of discarded police evidence tags, empty of their objects but heavy with implied stories. A single, chipped tile from the original Roman road, Cassel stamped on its back.

These were not forgeries. They were truths too small, too inconvenient, or too human for the official story. They were the dust that gathered in the seams of history.

His mind, trained in the logic of fakery, began to assemble them not into a lie, but into a weaponized anecdote. A story that would use the city’s own body—its stones, its archives, its forgotten whispers—against the story imposed upon it. He would not write it down. He would perform it. He would plant it, not in a portfolio, but in the fertile, paranoid soil of the public mind. He would make them see the glitch.

The first step was provenance. A forgery needed a credible origin. He needed a source, a shadow-authority. He lifted the wool guild ledger, its pages brittle as autumn leaves. The bribes were recorded in a cryptic, personal shorthand. He could amplify that. He could take the language of a 15th-century graft and extrapolate it, evolve it into a secret, continuous language of corruption, stretching from the medieval wool halls to the modern-day municipal data servers. A “Cipher of the Compromise.” He would not create a full codex. He would create fragments. A line scrawled on the back of a tram ticket left on a bench. A sequence of numbers etched with a diamond-tipped scribe into the base of a public statue, visible only to one kneeling. A phrase buried in the metadata of a digitized civic document, a ghost in the machine.

The second step was the relic. The tangible, physical heart of the story. His eyes fell on the Roman tile. Cassel. It was real. He would make it a key. Not to a treasure, but to a pattern. He spent an hour with his scrapers and a pot of acid-wash, not aging it, but clarifying it. He gave the stamp a microscopic, anachronistic detail—a symbol that echoed the logo of the consolidation’s holding company. A coincidence so blatant it would scream of hidden meaning. He would place this tile where it would be found by someone whose discovery would be unquestioned: a city archaeologist on a routine survey, a child digging in a park. The first breadcrumb.

The third and final step was the catalyst. The event that would fuse the provenance and the relic into a living, breathing conspiracy. It needed to be public. It needed to be inexplicable within their narrative. It needed to be a spectacle of meaningless meaning.

He knew then what he must do. He must forge a miracle.

Not a divine one. A bureaucratic one. A systemic hiccup so poetically resonant it would suggest a consciousness, a mocking intelligence within the city’s very infrastructure.

He packed a small bag: the tile, a data-stick loaded with corrupted metadata files, a vial of his most resilient, UV-visible ink, a set of lock-picks, and the bone folder. He extinguished the lantern. The darkness returned, complete, a velvet null. He was no longer Pieter van der Meer, forger. He was a narrative pathogen, preparing for transmission.

He moved upward, through strata of stone and time, emerging at last into the predawn of Ghent. The air was a cold, grey rinse. The city was a silhouette, its spires and gables cut from black paper against a slowly lightening sky. It was the hour of cleaners and bakers, of the night’s last ghosts and the day’s first laborers. The hour of the unseen.

He made his way to the Korenmarkt. The square was empty, the café chairs stacked like skeletal sentinels. The great, illuminated facade of the Sint-Niklaaskerk loomed, its stone lacework glowing under the security lights. His target was not the church, but the monument before it: the modernistic, bronze “Tree of Knowledge” fountain, a civic art installation funded by a philanthropic arm of the very conglomerate driving the consolidation. It was a symbol of transparent, benevolent progress.

From his bag, he took the vial of ink. It was a formula of his own devising, invisible under ordinary light, but which would fluoresce a faint, eerie green under the specific wavelength of the ultraviolet lamps used by the city’s night-time security drones. He uncapped it. The ink had no smell. It was a ghost of a liquid.

He approached the fountain’s dry basin. With the steady, practiced hand of a manuscript illuminator, he began to paint. Not words. A circuit diagram. A flow chart. It connected stylized representations of the church, the belfry, the modern atrium, and the fountain itself. Lines of influence, data streams, control nodes. At the center, where the “Tree’s” roots would be, he drew the symbol from the Roman tile. The design was a perfect hybrid of medieval alchemical notation and modern infographic logic. It was a map of a conspiracy that did not exist, rendered in a language that felt hauntingly familiar.

He finished as the first hint of peach tinged the eastern sky. The ink was invisible. The fountain looked pristine. He placed the Roman tile, symbol-side up, in the exact center of the diagram, mortaring it swiftly with a dab of quick-setting, stone-colored putty. It looked like a piece of routine maintenance, a small repair.

He retreated to the shadow of a colonnade as the city began to stir. A delivery van rumbled across the cobbles. A shopkeeper rolled up a grate. The first drone of the morning swept past, a silent, black beetle scanning for anomalies. Its UV light washed over the fountain basin for a fraction of a second. In Pieter’s mind’s eye, he saw the hidden diagram flare to life, a sudden, cryptic mandala of green fire, captured in the drone’s data stream before vanishing. An ephemeral ghost in the machine’s own perception.

It was done. The seed was planted. The provenance was the city’s own hidden history. The relic was in place. The catalyst would be the drone’s anomalous scan, which would trigger a maintenance alert, which would lead a puzzled city worker to the tile, which would be seen by the archaeologist, which would connect to the “Cipher” fragments he would begin disseminating in the days to come. The story would build itself, a self-assembling myth of resistance, a phantom narrative haunting the official one.

He felt no triumph. Only a profound, chilling emptiness. This was the ultimate forgery. He was not counterfeiting an object, but reality’s interpretation. He was polluting the well of meaning itself. He was no longer a man fighting a corrupt power. He was a man weaponizing absurdity. He had become the very chaos he had once labored, in his artful way, to conceal.

As the sun finally cleared the rooftops, flooding the Korenmarkt with a light that was the colour of pale sherry, he saw her. She stood on the far side of the square, near the tram lines, not looking at him. The woman from the cable chamber. She wore a different coat, but the posture was unmistakable—a contained, ready stillness. She held a small paper cup of coffee. She did not drink it. She simply stood, a fixed point in the flow of early commuters.

She was not there to apprehend him. Her presence was a statement. A receipt. We see you. We are the context in which you exist. Your glitch is contained within our system.

Pieter did not move from the shadows. He met her gaze across the bustling square. A silent, absolute recognition passed between them. The war had indeed descended to a more profound level. It was no longer a duel of spy and spy-master, of thief and warden. It was a duel of authors. She, the author of the clean, totalizing narrative of control. He, the author of the corrosive, viral anecdote.

He had forced the machine to wear a clumsier mask. Now, he would make the mask speak in tongues.

He turned and melted into the narrow street that led towards the Patershol, his figure dissolving into the shifting mosaic of shadow and light. Behind him, in the clean new daylight, the bronze Tree of Knowledge stood silent. Beneath it, hidden in plain sight, the first syllable of a corrosive whisper waited to be spoken. The contagion was no longer in the documents, nor solely in the lie. It was in the space between the story and the world. And Pieter van der Meer, forger and ghost, had just learned to breathe in that space.

He was patient zero. And the fever was beginning to spread.



---

Chapter 20

CHAPTER 20

The rain on the canal was the colour of tarnished pewter, and it fell not in drops but in a fine, persistent mist that erased the line between water and air. Pieter van der Meer stood under the limestone arch of a bridge in the Patershol, the stone weeping cold moisture onto the shoulders of his coat. He was not hiding. He was listening. The city’s morning symphony—the grumble of trams, the slap of wet newspaper against doorsteps, the low chatter of market stalls being erected—was a blanket of sound. Beneath it, he tuned his ear to the discordant notes: the too-regular footfall that did not vary with the cobbles, the static crackle of a radio earpiece disguised as a hearing aid, the soft, digital chime of a device logging a biometric ping.

They were herding him. The woman from the square was not the hunter; she was the beater, flushing game towards a prepared gun. Her presence at the Korenmarkt had been a deliberate nudge, a correction to his trajectory. He had expected containment, a swift, silent extraction from the flow. This was different. This was observation with a purpose. They were letting him run, but within a channel of their design. The realization was a cold stone in his gut. His viral anecdote was being catalogued, its propagation vectors mapped. To weaponize absurdity, one required a sane world to rebel against. They were preparing to absorb the absurd, to make it a feature of their system, not a bug.

He moved from the arch, his shoes whispering on the slick stones. The Patershol was a labyrinth of medieval humility, a nest of leaning houses and suffocating alleys built by the begging orders. It was a place of shadows so deep they felt archival. He slipped into a genever café that smelled of juniper and damp wool. At a corner table, he ordered a coffee he would not drink and opened a newspaper. The print was a grey smear. His eyes scanned not the headlines but the reflections in the flyblown mirror behind the bar.

He waited for the pattern to assert itself. It did.

A man in a municipal maintenance uniform, too clean, lingered by a gabled doorway opposite. A woman with a dog—a restless, whippet-thin thing that strained at its lead, its nose not to the ground but to the air—paused at the head of the alley. They were not closing. They were reinforcing the channel. Pieter felt a perverse admiration for the choreography. It was a forgery of inattention, a masterful copy of urban indifference. But like all forgeries, however exquisite, it contained the forger’s anxiety, the over-careful stroke. Their stillness was too perfect, their observation too patient. They were waiting for him to lead them to the source of the contagion.

He had no source. That was the point. The contagion was in the space between. But they, authors of a totalizing narrative, could not conceive of a story without an author. They needed a patient zero, a root, a cell to isolate. They believed he was it. And so, they would follow him until he led them to the non-existent laboratory, the empty plot, the hollow core of the joke.

A plan, cold and sharp as a burin, began to form in the acid bath of his mind. If they required an author, he would give them one. But not himself. He would forge a ghost, a phantom author, and lead them on a chase through the cathedral of someone else’s guilt.

He left coins on the table, the sound flat and final. Exiting not through the front but through the kitchen, he exchanged a silent nod with the old proprietor, a man who asked no questions of those who respected the sanctity of the back door. He emerged into a pinched courtyard strung with laundry, the sheets hanging limp and heavy in the mist like abandoned sails. From there, he descended into the city’s lower intestine.

The underground of Ghent was not a single layer but a palimpsest of neglect. Beneath the 19th-century sewers lay medieval cesspits, forgotten crypts, the silted-in beds of streams that had once defined the city’s shape. Pieter navigated by a cartography of memory and stench. He moved through a brick vault where the air was warm and thick with the yeasty exhalation of the breweries above, then down an iron ladder into a colder darkness that smelled of stone and slow decay. Here, the sounds of the city were a distant rumble, a memory of a world above.

In a niche formed by a collapsed arch, he kept a cache. Not a base of operations—he was too careful for that—but a palette of raw materials. A waterproof satchel contained a change of clothing, generic and dark, a bottle of distilled water, packets of hardtack. And the tools: a set of lockpicks finer than a surgeon’s probes, a brass-bound notebook with blank, cream-coloured pages, and a small, custom-made tablet computer, its case scratched and dented, utterly disconnected from any network. It was a monk’s cell for the digital age.

He powered the tablet. Its glow was a blasphemous moon in the absolute dark. On it, he accessed not files, but fragments. Photographs of archives he had visited years ago, details of municipal watermarks, the personal seal of a long-dead city clerk, the precise shade of fading bureaucratic ink. He began to compose.

He was not forging a document. He was forging a biography. A person.

His name would be Arjan de Wit. A mid-level archivist in the city’s Department of Public Works, circa 1987. A man of quiet routine, invisible by design. Pieter constructed him from the paper-dust outwards. He gave him a pension number, a former address in a since-demolished block near the Dampoort, a membership to a now-defunct philatelic society. He imagined his handwriting: a tight, economical script, born of a need to conserve space on crowded forms. He imagined his grievance—not a grand political fury, but a slow, corroding resentment against the systemic destruction of the city’s historical fabric in the name of progress. A man who watched the steam shovels gnaw at medieval foundations to lay modern cable, who saw original cobbles pried up and replaced with anonymous asphalt.

Arjan de Wit would have had access. He would have seen the early plans for the city’s digital nerve system. And he would have begun, in his fastidious, invisible way, to sabotage them. Not with bombs, but with fiction. He would have salted the archives with false references, created phantom infrastructure on official schematics, buried whispers in the foundational paperwork. A secret author, planting seeds of dysfunction that would only germinate decades later, in the age of total connectivity.

Pieter worked with the cold fury of an artist possessed. Using the tablet and a slender stylus, he generated scans of 1980s departmental stationery, the paper yellowed at the edges, the ink slightly blurred from a dot-matrix printer. He composed memos from Arjan de Wit to superiors, politely questioning alignment specifications. He created a personal diary, entries brief and bleak, detailing the “soul-sickness” of watching the old city be “catalogued and strangled.” He forged a termination notice, citing “psychological instability” and “persistent delusional behaviour regarding city infrastructure.” The final entry was a single line: They have made a map of the world that leaves no room for ghosts. I will become a ghost in their map.

It was a masterpiece of context. Every detail was plausible, rooted in the mundane reality of a fading municipal bureaucracy. It was a perfect, tragic, and entirely fictional explanation for the glitches now plaguing the system. It gave the authorities a narrative they could understand: a lone, disgruntled actor, now surely dead, whose legacy of petty sabotage was only now bearing strange fruit. It was a coffin for their inquiry.

But a forgery must be discovered, not presented. It must bear the patina of accidental finding. For that, he needed a repository. A grave for Arjan de Wit.

He knew the place. The old municipal archives had moved to a sterile, climate-controlled facility on the outskirts in the late 1990s. The original building, a damp neo-Gothic pile near the Vrijdagmarkt, had been sold to a property developer and left to rot, its contents supposedly emptied. But Pieter, in his earlier, more conventional researches, had discovered that not all the transfer orders were completed. Sub-basements were sealed, forgotten. Rooms filled with the detritus of a century of civic administration were simply bricked up, a tomb for obsolete paper.

He emerged from the underworld as the grey day bled into a greyer twilight. The rain had settled into a drizzle. He was now dressed as a utility worker, in a high-visibility vest over a dark jacket, carrying a heavy plastic toolbox. He moved with the weary purpose of a man on a late shift. The channel was still there—a van with tinted windows idling two streets over, a cyclist with a bulky backpack—but their focus had shifted. They were waiting for him to lead them to a person, a meeting. He would lead them to a corpse.

The old archive building loomed behind a fence plastered with fading permits and artist’s impressions of future luxury apartments. He found the breach he had used before, a section of chain-link peeled back and re-fastened with subtle wire hooks. Inside, the grandeur of decay awaited. A vaulted entrance hall was now a forest of scaffolding. Plaster cherubs lay shattered on the marble floor, their faces blank with dust. The air was a cocktail of mildew, pigeon droppings, and the ghost of leaded ink.

He ignored the grand staircases and found a servants’ passage, a tight spiral of stairs leading down. The lower levels were a kingdom of damp. Water gleamed on cracked tile. His flashlight beam cut through the gloom, picking out the skeletons of steel filing cabinets, their drawers vomited forth cascades of water-swollen paper, a papier-mâché avalanche frozen mid-fall. He moved with deliberate noise, kicking a piece of pipe, letting his toolbox clang against a doorframe. He wanted the sound to carry. He wanted to be followed.

In the deepest sub-basement, behind a door marked “BOILER – NO ADMITTANCE,” was the room. It was a long, low vault, its walls lined with wooden pigeonholes, each stuffed with bound folders. This was the forgotten annex, the records of the Public Works Department from 1975 to 1995. A mausoleum of civic inertia.

He went to work. Selecting a dusty, secluded carrel at the room’s far end, he placed his creation. He did not simply leave the folder. He staged a scene. He opened the brass-bound notebook to a random page and placed a cheap, erasable pen beside it, as if recently set down. He arranged the forged memos and the diary as if they had been pulled from a file for review. He even sprinkled a fine layer of dust from the floor over the top sheets, then carefully blew most of it away, leaving only a trace in the crevices, the impression of recent disturbance. He placed the personnel termination notice on top, the final, damning piece.

Then, he took from his toolbox the final, crucial artifact: a pair of wire-rimmed spectacles, lenses slightly scratched, one temple mended with a tiny piece of electrical tape. He had purchased them from a second-hand stall a year ago. He laid them gently atop the folder, one lens over the name Arjan de Wit. The personal touch. The relic.

It was done. The ghost was laid to rest in a fitting sepulchre.

He retreated to a shadowed alcove behind a collapsed shelf unit, a vantage point that gave him a view of the door. He extinguished his light and became part of the darkness, his breathing slowing to match the slow drip of water somewhere in the pipes. The cold of the stone seeped through his clothes. He waited.

They did not make him wait long.

The door opened without a sound. A blade of white light from a powerful torch sliced through the gloom, probing the aisles. It was held by the woman from the cable chamber. She had traded her coat for a black tactical jacket. Behind her, two figures moved in the darkness, their outlines blocky with equipment. They were professionals. They cleared the room with silent, efficient sweeps, their lights never crossing, covering each other’s angles.

The woman’s beam found the carrel. It lingered on the scattered papers, the notebook, the glasses. She approached, not with haste, but with a predatory grace. She stopped a foot away, her light fixed on the folder. One of her companions moved to cover the door. The other began a thermal scan of the room. Pieter held his breath, willing his body heat to dissipate into the ancient stone.

The woman did not touch the papers immediately. She observed. She took a small device from her pocket and scanned the area, presumably for electronic signatures. Finding none, she finally, using a gloved hand, lifted the termination notice. Her torchlight illuminated the forged ink. She read it. Then she read the final diary entry. She stood perfectly still for a long moment.

Pieter, from his hiding place, watched her face. In the backscatter of the torchlight, he saw not triumph, not satisfaction, but a profound and weary recognition. She saw the narrative laid out before her: the sad, petty, comprehensible truth. It was a cleaner story than the one Pieter was living. It had a beginning, a middle, and an end. It could be filed, explained, and closed. It was the story her world was built to digest.

She spoke, her voice low and carried by the room’s acoustics to his hiding place. “It’s him. The source. De Wit.” She sounded almost disappointed.

One of the men spoke into a throat mic. “Asset is contained. Proceeding with collection and sanitization.”

They began to meticulously bag the evidence, treating it with the reverence of archaeologists. The glasses went into a separate padded container. The woman stood back, overseeing. Her torch beam swept the room once more, passing over Pieter’s alcove. It paused for a fraction of a second on the darkness where he stood. He felt the light like a physical pressure. Did she see the deeper shadow within the shadow? Did some part of her, the author sensing a competing voice, perceive the true forger watching from the wings?

Her beam moved on.

“Clear,” she said, her tone definitive. “The narrative is secure. Let’s close the environment.”

They left as silently as they had come, taking the ghost of Arjan de Wit with them. Pieter remained in the darkness for a full hour after the last echo of their footsteps had faded. He listened to the old building settle around him. He had succeeded. He had given them their author, their contained, resolved story. He should have felt the cold thrill of victory.

Instead, he felt a hollowing dread. He had watched the machine ingest his fiction without a hiccup. It had accepted the forged biography as truth, not because it was flawless, but because it was convenient. It fit the template. The system’s immunity was not based on detecting falsehood, but on metabolizing all stories into its own sustaining narrative. He had not corrupted the well of meaning. He had simply added another cup of water to a well that was already bottomless, already indifferent.

The war was not between authors. It was between authorship and a void that wore the mask of authorship. She and her kind were not storytellers. They were curators of an eternal, self-justifying exhibit. And he had just donated a perfectly crafted piece to their collection.

He emerged into the night. The rain had stopped. The cobbles gleamed under the sodium streetlights like wet scales. He was free. The channel was gone; the herding pressure had vanished. They had their root cause. Patient zero had been identified, catalogued, and filed away. Pieter van der Meer was, for now, irrelevant.

He walked towards the Leie, the river a black ribbon stitched with reflections of bridge lights. The victory was ashes. He had traded pursuit for oblivion. To be hunted was to be acknowledged. To be dismissed as a symptom of a dead man’s spite was to be erased.

As he crossed the Grasbrug, he saw the new bronze Tree of Knowledge on the Korenmarkt, illuminated by discreet spotlights. It stood as a monument to transparent truth. And beneath it, he knew, the first syllable of his whisper—the real contagion, the one in the space between—still waited. But it was a whisper in a cathedral that had just proven it could silence any echo it chose to hear.

A figure detached itself from the shadows at the far end of the bridge. It was not her. It was an old man, bundled in a shabby coat, leaning on a cane. As Pieter drew nearer, the man raised his head. It was the proprietor of the genever café. His eyes, in the half-light, were not the eyes of a neutral observer. They held a sharp, evaluating intelligence.

He did not speak. He simply let something fall from his folded hand. A small, dense object that clinked once on the wet stone at Pieter’s feet before rolling to a stop.

Pieter looked down. It was a key. Not a modern Yale key, but an old, heavy skeleton key, its bow wrought in the shape of a closed book.

The old man turned and shuffled away, his cane tapping a slow, deliberate rhythm on the cobbles, a counterpoint to the city’s digital heartbeat. He disappeared into an alley.

Pieter stood over the key. His forged narrative was sealed in an evidence bag, on its way to becoming official truth. His pursuers had turned their eyes away. He was a ghost again. But this key was not for a ghost. It was for a reader. Someone who understood that the most dangerous stories are not the ones told to the authorities, but the ones passed, silently, from one author to another, in the spaces the map leaves blank.

He bent and picked it up. The metal was cold, older than the city’s new systems. It weighed heavily in his palm.

The fever had not spread. It had been quarantined. But in the moment of its containment, a different signal had been sent, on a frequency he had not known to monitor. The duel was not over. It had merely changed venues. He was no longer patient zero. He was an invitation. And the lock that awaited this key was somewhere in the dark, patient belly of the world, where the maps ended and the true margins began.



---

Chapter 21

CHAPTER 21

The key was a cold tooth against his palm, a fossilised bite from a world that ran on different physics. Pieter stood on the bridge, the city’s digital hum a gauzy veil over the older, deeper silence. The genever man’s tap-tap-tap had faded, absorbed by the alley’s throat. The gesture was not an offer of alliance. It was a test of literacy. A man who could forge a Mercator, who could weave a contagion of plausible lies, would know how to read a blank page. This key was the first line on such a page.

He did not go home. Home was a set, a stage dressed for a performance that had, officially, reached its curtain. The evidence bag containing his forged confession was a closed book in the eyes of the state. His apartment, with its calculated disarray, was now a museum to a ghost. To return would be to perform for an empty house.

Instead, he walked, letting the key’s weight in his coat pocket pull him through the capillaries of the city. The rain had softened to a mist that beaded on the wool of his shoulders and silvered the cobbles under the sporadic glow of sensor-lights. He was a reader tracing a single, heavy character through a text of noise and shadow. The power he had manipulated—the corrupt, lazy power of institutions that preferred a neat lie to a messy truth—had turned its great eye away. But corruption was not a monolithic force. It was fractal. It repeated itself in ever-smaller, ever-sharper iterations, down to the level of the single transaction, the silent exchange in a wet street. The genever man was a node in a different network, one that traded not in data or jurisdiction, but in artefacts of meaning. Pieter had passed their first test: he had created a story potent enough to be noticed, and discreet enough to be contained. Now they offered a lexicon.

He found himself, hours later, in the Patershol, the labyrinth of medieval streets where the buildings leaned close enough to whisper secrets across the gaps. The air smelled of damp mortar and fried dough. Here, the city’s new nervous system grew faint; the CCTV cameras were tourists, blind to the grammar of the place. He stopped before a door that was not a door. It was a recessed panel of blackened oak, set between a shuttered bakery and a gallery selling neon tchotchkes to students. There was no handle, no lockplate. Only a vertical seam in the wood, and above it, set into the stone lintel, a small, eroded carving: a book with its covers shut.

He drew the key from his pocket. The bow, the closed book, was a perfect match to the carving, though the stone was worn by centuries of weather and the metal was dark with the sweat of palms long dead. He did not look for a keyhole. He pressed the cold metal against the carving.

For a long moment, nothing. Then a sound, not from the door but from the stone itself—a deep, granular click, like a molar settling in a giant’s jaw. The seam in the oak widened by a hair’s breadth. He pushed, and the panel swung inward on hinges that made no protest, swallowing sound as a vault swallows light.

Beyond was darkness, and the smell of old paper, beeswax, and stone dust. Pieter stepped over the threshold, and the door sealed itself behind him with a soft, final exhalation. The digital hum of Ghent vanished. The silence here was absolute, a pressure on the eardrums.

He stood still, letting his eyes adjust. A faint, sourceless luminescence seeped into the space, revealing a long, narrow room—a corridor, really—with a vaulted brick ceiling. It was a crypt for books. Shelves cut from raw black stone held not volumes, but portfolios, caskets, cylindrical cases of tarnished copper and cracked leather. There were no titles, no labels. Only a patina of profound, patient neglect.

At the far end, a single pool of light fell from a green-shaded brass lamp onto a broad stone table. Seated behind it, her hands resting on a closed folio of mottled calfskin, was the woman from the bridge. The one who had not come. She was older than the mist and the rain had suggested, or perhaps ageless in the way of certain hardwoods. Her hair, the colour of tarnished silver, was swept back from a face that was all acute angles and watchful calm. She wore a simple, high-collared jacket of charcoal wool. Her eyes, in the lamplight, were the colour of the North Sea under a winter sky.

“Mijnheer van der Meer,” she said. Her voice was dry, precise, without echo in the absorbent dark. “You are punctual. A forgery must be, above all, timely. Come. Sit.”

He approached, the sound of his shoes on the flagstones the only noise in the world. He took the worn leather chair opposite her. The folio before her was closed with a leather strap and a simple brass clasp.

“You are not with the authorities,” he said. It was not a question.

“The authorities are children playing with blocks,” she replied. “They build towers of fact. They topple them with scandals. They believe the world is made of these towers. We know it is made of the spaces between.” She tapped a finger, long and pale, on the folio. “You engineered a space. A beautiful, contained paradox. You gave them a truth so perfectly shaped to their expectations that it became, for them, inarguable. You painted over a window and called it a wall, and they thanked you for the masonry. That is a form of power they cannot comprehend, because it does not seek to dominate, but to edit.”

“We,” Pieter echoed.

“A loose confederation of editors,” she said, a ghost of a smile touching her lips. “Librarians of the unrecorded. Curators of the plausible deniability. You have been practising our craft in isolation. A talented autodidact. Your Mercator was… competent. The vellum was a nice touch. The iron gall ink, brewed to the correct acidity to mimic the passage of centuries. But you think like a painter. You create a finished image.”

She unclasped the folio and opened it. Inside were not pages, but layers of transparent overlays—vellum, linen, fine parchment—each bearing a fragment of a map. A coastline here, a rhumb line there, a cartouche, a sea-monster. They were layered, misaligned, a palimpsest of potential geographies.

“We do not forge documents, Mijnheer. We forge context. A single, perfect lie is a diamond: brilliant, fragile, and ultimately useless. A network of suggestive half-truths, of plausible echoes, is a mycelium. It feeds on the rot of certainty. It fruits in the dark places where the official story cannot grow.”

Pieter looked at the layered fragments. He understood. It was the difference between counterfeiting a banknote and subtly shifting the economic theory that underpinned the currency. A deeper, more corrosive authorship.

“Why show me this?”

“Because you are an invitation that has been accepted,” she said, closing the folio. “Your performance with the contagion protocol was audacious. It drew the eye of the wrong people. And the right ones. The man in the café is one of our… lookouts. He filters the noise for signals of a certain frequency. You transmitted. We are replying.”

She leaned forward, the lamplight carving deeper shadows into her face. “The power you manipulated is a symptom. The corruption you exploited is a surface stain. Beneath it is the engine: a project, not of a government, but of a consortium. Private capital, legacy institutions, certain academic factions. They are not building towers. They are building a canon. A single, authorised, immutable version of reality. Digitised, catalogued, algorithmically enforced. A world where every artifact, every historical event, every cultural memory has one entry, one interpretation, one truth-value. A world without margins. Without whispers.”

Pieter felt a cold that had nothing to do with the crypt. He thought of the quiet, relentless digitisation in the museum basements, the substitution of metadata for mystery. “A monopoly on meaning.”

“Precisely. Their corruption is not financial, though money fuels it. It is epistemological. It is the corruption of the very possibility of a secret, of a dissent, of a story that belongs only to those who tell it. Your little contagion was a splinter in their system. A proof of concept that their sanitised world could still be infected by a well-told lie. They contained it, yes. But they saw it. And now they will seek to inoculate. To close the last gaps. They have a key artifact, a linchpin for their first major exhibition of this ‘verified past.’ They call it the Codex Unitas.”

She drew a photograph from a drawer and slid it across the stone. It showed a book, large and formidable, bound in dark leather over wooden boards, studded with heavy brass clasps. It lay on a velvet cushion in a sterile, high-security lab.

“It is purported to be a 15th-century concordance, a unified index of heretical texts compiled by a shadow council of bishops before the Reformation. A taxonomy of dissent. Its provenance is, of course, flawless. Carbon-dated, ink-analysed, historically vetted. It is the ultimate weapon for their canon: an ancient, ‘objective’ authority that categorises and thereby neuters every historical alternative. It makes rebellion a filed, labelled specimen. Once it is unveiled and authenticated by their chorus of experts, the foundation is laid. The past becomes a locked room, and they hold the only key.”

Pieter studied the photograph. The book had a dreadful, inert authority. “And it is a forgery.”

“Of course it is,” she said, her voice edged with frost. “A magnificent one. Perhaps the greatest of our age. Created by a man named Elias Thorne. He was one of us. A master. He understood that to combat a canon, one must not destroy it, but pre-empt it. He created the Codex not as a simple fake, but as a Trojan horse. Within its ‘authentic’ pages are layers of coded contradictions, subtle anachronisms of pigment and phrasing, a skeleton of lies waiting to be articulated. But Thorne disappeared three months ago. Before he could activate the codex’s flaws. Before he could give us the key to unravel it.”

The silence stretched, thick with implication.

“You think they have him,” Pieter said.

“We know they do. He is not dead. His knowledge is too specific, too valuable. They will be… persuading him. To confirm the Codex’s authenticity, to silence its built-in contradictions. To turn his masterpiece into their foundation stone. They have a facility. Off-grid. A former monastic archive near the coast, now a private research centre. A black site for history.”

She pushed a second photograph toward him. It was an aerial shot, grainy, of a stark, fortified complex built around a central tower, surrounded by salt marshes. It looked like a prison for ghosts.

“Your task, should you choose to accept this editing,” she said, her gaze holding his, “is not to destroy the Codex. Destruction is a child’s protest. Your task is to complete Thorne’s work. To enter that place. To find him, or what remains of him. To extract the key—the grammatical key to his forgery—and then to use it. To edit the Codex from within, at the moment of its supreme triumph. To turn their cornerstone into a sinkhole.”

Pieter looked from the photograph of the grim complex to the layered folio of map fragments. The duel had indeed changed venues. He had graduated from fooling bureaucrats to infiltrating a fortress of curated reality. The power he faced was no longer a corrupt official, but the architect of a corruption so profound it sought to become the air that all future minds would breathe.

“Why me? I am a map forger, not a commando.”

“Because you are a ghost they have just officially buried,” she said. “Because you understand that every fortress has a blind spot—not in its security, but in its story. They will have a narrative for that place, a provenance. A water-treatment plant. A private agri-research station. You will forge the credentials to match that story. You will not break in. You will be invited in. You will be the expert they didn’t know they needed, until you present yourself. And because…” She paused, and for the first time, he saw a flicker of something like remorse in her marine eyes. “Thorne admired your work. The Mercator. He said it had ‘the correct loneliness.’ He believed you were the only one who might think enough like him to find the key, if he cannot speak it.”

She reached into a drawer and placed a small, sealed tube of black aluminium on the table. “A data needle. Empty. It will read the Codex’s structural flaws, if you can access its digital scan-server. It will also, if you find him, read Thorne’s neural imprint—a memory palace extraction—if he is capable of giving it. It is your pen. Your ink.”

Pieter picked up the cold tube. It weighed nothing. It weighed everything. He was no longer an invitation. He was a sentence being inserted into a hostile text.

“And if I fail?”

“Then the Codex Unitas becomes truth,” she said simply. “And the margins in which we work, the spaces between the towers, begin to close. For good. Your forgery will stand as your last, and only, testament. A beautiful, lonely lie in a world that has voted for a single, seamless truth.”

He sat back, the cold stone of the crypt at his spine. The fever of his own making was gone, quarantined in an evidence locker. But here, in this silent belly of the world, a colder, slower sickness was thriving. And the only vaccine was a deeper, more perfect lie. He was not a saviour. He was a syntactical correction. A editor entering the press room of God.

“I will need materials,” he said, his voice quiet in the consuming dark. “A history. A provenance. A reason to be at that door.”

The woman nodded, the ghost-smile returning. “We have already begun the draft.”

She rose, taking the folio and the photographs, leaving only the data needle on the stone. “The door will remain responsive to your key. Come when you are ready to begin the edit. Do not rush. Authenticity is a matter of patience.”

She turned and melted into the darkness beyond the lamplight, her footsteps making no sound. Pieter was alone in the library of unrecorded things. He picked up the data needle, its surface slick and featureless. He had spent his life learning the texture of old vellum, the smell of ancient ink, the weight of a plausible past. Now he would have to learn the texture of a lie so large it intended to digest the future. The lock that awaited his key was not a physical mechanism. It was the mind of the world, and he had just agreed to pick it.



---

Chapter 22

CHAPTER 22

The silence in the crypt library was not an absence, but a substance. It was the cold, distilled residue of secrets kept, of histories deemed too dangerous for the light. Pieter sat within it, the data needle a smooth, alien shard in his palm. It held no warmth, offered no tactile confession. It was the antithesis of his craft. He did not work in binaries and clean light, but in the granular, imperfect shadows of animal skin and mineral stain. To forge a memory—not on parchment, but within the wetware of a man, within the sanctioned archives of the world—was to move from the palette of the earthly to that of the divine. Or the demonic.

He rose, his joints protesting the crypt’s chill and his own stillness. The single lamp guttered, its flame starving in the processed air. He left it burning, a tiny, defiant organ of warmth in the architectural corpse. The door to the spiral stair sighed open at his approach, recognizing the key he had become. He climbed, leaving the library of unrecorded things below, feeling its weight settle on his shoulders like a mantle of lead.

His own room in the safe-house was a cell of curated authenticity. It was not the scholarly disarray of his Ghent atelier; that had been a performance for a world that believed in the romance of solitary genius. This was a workshop for a more surgical kind of deceit. On a steel table lay the “materials” the woman had promised. A slim dossier contained the history of one Dr. Alistair Meier, a senior archivist for the Codex Unitas’s Historical Verification Corps, currently on medical leave for a chronic neural-interface migraine disorder. There was a photograph. Meier had Pieter’s build, his colouring, but his face was softer, blurred by administrative years and the faint, permanent crease of screen-fatigue between his brows. Pieter studied it, not to memorize features, but to absorb the man’s posture of weary authority. The provenance of his false self.

Beside the dossier lay the tools of Meier’s trade, and now his: a grey verification officer’s uniform, its fabric a static-dissipating weave; a pair of plain, wire-framed glasses that were, in fact, a low-grade cortical scanner; and a verification slate, a cold porcelain rectangle that would grant him tiered access to the Codex servers. It was blank, inert. It awaited the data needle.

Pieter picked up the slate. It was lighter than vellum, heavier than sin. He inserted the needle into its port. There was no sound, no light. But the slate’s surface warmed infinitesimally, and a single line of text glowed into being, a pale blue ghost in the porcelain.

Primary Objective: Locate and extract Subject Thorne (Elias). Neural imprint protocol authorized. Secondary: Plant construct ‘Cartographer’s Lament’ in server node Theta-7.

He put the slate down. The words were a clinical incantation. They did not speak of a man, but of a subject. They did not speak of a forgery, but of a construct. This was the language of the towers, clean and bloodless. It was a language he would have to learn to speak without accent.

For three days, he did not leave the room. He became Alistair Meier. He studied the man’s service record, his published verification notes on 17th-century mercantile ledgers, his dietary preferences (yeast-free, high in synthetic protein), the specific cadence of his speech harvested from old staff meeting recordings—a slight hesitation before nouns, as if double-checking their validity. Pieter practiced the walk: not the confident stride of a curator, but the shuffling, careful gait of a man whose world was a labyrinth of fragile, priceless data. He learned the migraine tic, the pressing of thumb and forefinger at the bridge of the nose, a gesture of pain that would also conveniently shield his eyes from retinal scanners.

On the morning of the fourth day, he dressed in Meier’s uniform. The fabric whispered against his skin, a sound like falling sand. He placed the glasses on his face. The world gained a faint, digital grid, a whisper of metadata hovering at the edge of perception. The verification slate, slipped into his inner pocket, lay against his ribs like a second, colder heart.

The woman met him at the door to the surface access tunnel. She wore a different face today, sharper, with the competent anonymity of a mid-level infrastructure manager. She handed him a transit pass and a small, foil-wrapped tablet.

“For the migraines,” she said, her voice now higher, tighter. “Your biometrics are queued. The medical leave is rescinded as of 08:00 today, citing a successful experimental treatment. You are returning to duty early, eager to contribute to the final verification push for the North Sea sector. Your access is limited to public zones and your assigned verification chamber until you… find your way.”

Pieter took the tablet. “And the way in?”

“Is the same as the way out,” she said. “Through the door everyone uses. The front one.”

He understood. The greatest forgeries were not hidden in secret compartments. They were displayed in the main gallery, resting on the unquestioned authority of the plaque beside them. He was to be the plaque.

The tunnel led to a nondescript service elevator in a municipal parking complex. The elevator rose, humming a bland corporate tune, and opened into a lobby of polished basalt and muted light. This was not one of the great public entrances to the Codex Unitas spire, but a staff ingress, a pore in the monolith’s skin. A few other grey-uniformed figures moved through the security arch, their eyes glazed, their minds already subsumed by the day’s data-stream. Pieter joined the queue, adopting Meier’s weary slouch.

He presented his slate. The arch glowed a soft green. A synthetic voice, genderless and warm, said, “Welcome back, Dr. Meier. We are pleased you are feeling better. Proceed to Verification Chamber Theta-14 for your re-assignment briefing.”

The green light was a sacrament. It washed him in the legitimacy of the tower. He was inside.

The interior of the Codex Unitas was a cathedral to the annihilation of shadow. Light came from everywhere and nowhere, a seamless, shadowless illumination that rendered everything in a state of hyper-real clarity. The air was scentless, temperature-less. Sound was absorbed into the porous walls, leaving only the soft, insectile hum of servers and the whisper of soles on conductive flooring. It was the absolute opposite of the crypt, of his atelier. Here, nothing was permitted to hide. Here, every truth was meant to be visible, indexed, and linked.

It was, he realized with a forger’s cold eye, the perfect place to hide a lie. In such sterility, the smallest, sanctioned anomaly would become invisible.

He followed the gentle pulse of guidance lights in the floor to a bank of transparent tubes. A capsule sighed open. He entered, and it ascended not with a lurch, but with a seamless, constant acceleration that left his stomach behind. Through the clear walls, the interior of the spire unfolded—vast open atria stacked like glass honeycombs, filled with thousands of verification officers at their consoles, their faces bathed in the cool light of their slates. It was a vision of serene, total productivity. A hive mind at peace.

Verification Chamber Theta-14 was a small, hexagonal room with a single interface console. The door sealed behind him with a sound like a vacuum lock. A briefing was already unfolding on the main screen—a bland, animated presentation about updated protocols for cross-referencing digital maritime logs with surviving physical fragments. Pieter let it play, a drone of legitimacy, as he accessed the chamber’s terminal with Meier’s credentials.

His fingers, trained to feel the tooth of vellum, now danced over holographic keys. He navigated the Codex interior directories. They were a marvel of bureaucratic clarity, and thus, a labyrinth of profound obscurity. He searched for Elias Thorne. No results. He searched for neural imprint extraction records, for special detainment facilities, for archival quarantine zones. Access denied. Tier insufficient.

He was a junior editor in the press room of God, permitted only to check the spelling of minor saints.

He leaned back, pressing Meier’s migraine tic against his nose. The grid in his glasses flickered, overlaying a schematic of the local server network. He was in a sub-node. Somewhere above, perhaps hundreds of meters above in the spire’s gleaming pinnacle, was the core. And somewhere in the bowels, in the architectural subconscious of the structure, would be the places not listed on the clean schematic. The margins.

His slate vibrated. A new assignment packet auto-loaded. Priority Verification: Portolan Chart Fragment, Designation NS-77-44. Suspected Anomaly: Ink spectral analysis deviates from Mercator benchmark. Please reconcile or flag for deletion.

Attached was a high-resolution scan. It was a corner of his own forgery, the Mercator chart he had been perfecting in Ghent a lifetime ago. They had it. Of course they had it. It was in the evidence locker, part of the fever of his own making. Now it was here, a piece of data on a screen, awaiting verification. Its fate was to be reconciled—assimilated into the official truth—or deleted.

A hook, baited with his own life’s work.

He accepted the task. The system granted him temporary, elevated access to the Comparative Materials Database. It was a foot in a narrower door. He began to work, not as Pieter the forger, but as Dr. Meier the verifier. He called up spectral analyses of genuine Mercator inks, cross-referenced them with the scan. He noted the deviations—his deviations, the careful imperfections he had baked into the formula to mimic centuries of subtle decay. He wrote a preliminary note: Anomalies consistent with vellum biodegradation catalysts and regional humidity variance. Not indicative of forgery. Recommend reconciliation.

As he worked, he wrote a secondary script. A silent, sub-cutaneous query that piggybacked on every legitimate search. He was not searching for Thorne directly. He was searching for the silence around him. He looked for server clusters with disproportionately high energy draw but no corresponding data traffic. He looked for maintenance logs showing repeated service to sub-levels marked ‘architectural void’. He looked for personnel transfers to a department listed only as ‘Acquisitions & Integration’.

The data began to coalesce, not into an answer, but into a shape. A shape of absence. It pointed down.

His console pinged. A supervisor message. Dr. Meier. Your reconciliation on NS-77-44 is noted. Efficient. Given your proficiency with cartographic materials, you are temporarily reassigned to assist with a physical audit in Sub-Archive Theta-Gamma. Report to Conveyor Bay 7.

Physical audit. A place where data met dust. A place below.

Conveyor Bay 7 was a cavernous space where the sleekness of the upper spire gave way to functional grime. Here, automated carts on silent tracks carried sealed containers from the depths to the verification atria and back again. The air smelled of ozone and cold stone. A foreman with a bored expression handed him a slate with a manifest. “Theta-Gamma. Section nine. Spot-check the container tags against the master log. Look for degradation markers. Don’t touch the artifacts.”

The conveyor capsule this time descended, a long, smooth plunge into the spire’s roots. The light grew dimmer, tinged with a utilitarian yellow. The temperature dropped. When the door opened, it was onto a world of shadows.

Sub-Archive Theta-Gamma was a forest of towering metal racks, stretching into darkness. Each rack held hundreds of sealed, transparent sarcophagi, within which rested the physical originals of the world’s history: parchments, paintings, tablets, books. They were the corpses, here in the crypt, while their digital ghosts lived in the light above. It was a morgue for truth.

Pieter’s assigned section was devoted to early modern navigation. He moved down the narrow aisle, his slate’s glow a feeble lantern. He performed the mindless check, scanning tags, comparing numbers. The silence was profound, broken only by the distant hum of climate systems. Here, the shadowless light of the towers did not reach. Here, things could hide.

He found the anomaly in the log itself. A container, tagged P-0991, was listed on his manifest, but its entry in the master log was a string of corrupted symbols. Its location was listed as an alphanumeric code that corresponded to no rack number in the section schematic. He cross-referenced it with the shape of absence he’d built upstairs. The codes were adjacent.

He left his assigned aisle, walking deeper into the archive. The aisles became a maze. The lighting faltered. He followed the alphanumeric markers, a trail of breadcrumbs into a darker heart. He passed a junction where the architecture changed—the smooth metal gave way to older, poured concrete, stained with damp. He was passing from the Codex proper into something older, something it had been built upon.

The corridor ended at a heavy, neutral-grey door. There was no handle, no keypad. Only a small, dark lens at eye level. A neural scanner.

This was the lock. Not the one to the spire, but the one within it.

Pieter’s mouth was dry. He had no weapon, no tool but the lie he wore. He took off Meier’s glasses. He looked directly into the lens. He was not Dr. Alistair Meier, eager verifier. He was Pieter van der Meer, forger, invited guest, syntactical correction. He let the careful, weary mask fall away. He let the man who had warmed cognac in the Ghent twilight, who had brewed ink from oak gall and sin, rise to the surface. He thought of the cold weight of the data needle, of the woman’s ghost-smile in the crypt. He thought of a sentence being inserted into a hostile text.

He did not blink.

The lens remained dark for three long heartbeats. Then it glowed, a soft, amber light that was the first warm colour he had seen since entering the spire. It was the colour of a dying sun. Of cognac.

With a deep, hydraulic sigh, the door recessed and slid sideways into the wall.

The room beyond was small, circular, and lit by a single source: a pale blue column of light in its center, within which hung the translucent, three-dimensional ghost of a man. It was Elias Thorne. Or what remained of him. He was suspended, fetal, connected by a web of faint filaments of light to the column. His eyes were open, but they saw nothing in this room. They were fixed on the interior of his own mind, being slowly, meticulously unspooled.

Around the perimeter of the room stood three verification officers. But their uniforms were different—black, with subtle silver piping. They did not turn as Pieter entered. Their attention was on control panels, their fingers moving through holographic streams of data that flowed from the column. One of them spoke, his voice filtered and flat.

“Integration is at seventy-eight percent. The palace is extensive. Well-structured. The Cartography section is particularly deep. Proceeding with extraction of primary emotional nodes.”

They were not just reading his memory. They were mapping its architecture, preparing to mine it for the Codex. To integrate his truth, his unique, heretical understanding of the spaces between, into their seamless whole. They were verifying him to death.

Pieter’s slate vibrated against his ribs. The secondary objective. Plant construct ‘Cartographer’s Lament’ in server node Theta-7.

He understood now. Theta-7 was not a server. It was this room. The construct was not a data file. It was him.

He took a step forward. The officer monitoring the emotional node extraction half-turned. “This is a sealed integration chamber. Identify.”

Pieter did not use Meier’s voice. He used his own, quiet, stripped of all but intent. “I am the edit.”

He threw the verification slate. It was not aimed at the officer. It sailed, spinning, through the air towards the central column of light.

Everything happened in a suspended, crystalline moment.

The officer’s hand moved towards a sidearm.

The slate intersected the blue light.

And Pieter van der Meer, forger of worlds, spoke the words the woman had drilled into him, the words that were the key to the final lock, the syntax of the virus. They were not code. They were a line from a forgotten poem Thorne had once quoted, the emotional core of his cartographic obsession: “The truest map shows where the world ends, and the soul’s terrain begins.”

The slate detonated.

Not with fire, not with sound. With a pulse of nullity. A wave of perfect, absolute dark that swallowed the blue light, the holograms, the room’s illumination. It was the dark of the crypt, of the unrecorded, of the margin. It was a forgery of oblivion.

In the sudden, impenetrable black, Pieter moved. He was not a fighter. He was a ghost in a machine that had just gone blind. He heard shouts, the clatter of a sidearm hitting the floor, the sizzle of overloaded systems. He stumbled towards where the column had been, his hands outstretched.

His fingers touched not light, but cold, clammy flesh. Thorne. He found the web of filaments, tore at them. They came away like brittle spider silk. The body in the center slumped, a dead weight into Pieter’s arms.

A hand grabbed his shoulder from behind. He twisted, driving his elbow back into a soft stomach. He heard a grunt. He dragged Thorne, a sack of bones and ruined mind, towards where he remembered the door.

Emergency lights stuttered on, a hellish red strobe. One of the black-uniformed officers was on the floor, clutching his head. Another was fumbling with his weapon. The third was at his console, screaming into a comms link. “Theta-7 breach! Neural collapse! Initiating quarantine lockdown!”

The door was still open. The virus had frozen the systems. But not for long.

Pieter hauled Thorne through the doorway, back into the concrete corridor. As the door began to shudder, trying to close, he saw the officer with the weapon raise it, aim. Pieter turned his back, shielding Thorne’s body with his own.

The shot did not come. Instead, a new sound filled the corridor—a rising, deafening shriek of tearing metal from somewhere far above. The whole structure trembled. Dust rained from the ceiling. The red lights died, replaced by a deeper, systemic blackness.

The quarantine lockdown had triggered something else. A failure cascade. The spire’s perfect, shadowless heart had been injected with a pathogen of ancient, physical chaos.

In the absolute dark, Pieter van der Meer crouched over the body of Elias Thorne, in the belly of the world that had voted for a single, seamless truth. Above him, the tower was beginning to scream. And the edit, it seemed, was more profound than he had been led to believe. He had not just picked the lock of a mind.

He had pulled on a loose thread, and the entire tapestry was now unravelling in the dark.



---

Chapter 23

CHAPTER 23

The dark was not an absence. It was a substance. It filled Pieter’s mouth, his lungs, a fine particulate of pulverised concrete and dead dreams. It had weight, and taste—the alkaline grit of shattered gypsum, the copper tang of severed neural feeds, the underlying sweetness of ozone from burnt-out systems. He crouched in it, one hand splayed on the cold floor for balance, the other still clutching the rough wool of Elias Thorne’s jacket. The body was a anchor in the formless tide. A dead weight. A fact.

Above, the scream of the spire modulated. It was no longer a single, sustained shriek of protest, but a chorus of failures—a deep, groaning complaint of overstressed steel, the percussive snap of shearing bolts, the glassy rain of falling data-tiles. It was the sound of a mind, a vast and terrible mind, experiencing a stroke. Synapses firing into void. Pathways collapsing. The edit was indeed profound. He had not merely corrupted a file. He had introduced a syntax error into the language of reality this place was built upon. A forgery of chaos, etched onto the pristine vellum of their control.

Power corruption. He understood it now, not as an observation, but as a condition. A fever in the blood of the world. It had infected the architects of the spire, yes, with their clean, surgical truth. But it had also infected him. His was not the corruption of imposition, but of erasure. He had become the solvent applied to the masterpiece, the acid bloom eating through the layers of careful glaze until the original, ugly sketch beneath was revealed. He was the flaw in the document. The tremor in the hand that draws the line.

Thorne stirred. A wet, rattling inhalation that was more reflex than life. Pieter’s fingers found the man’s throat, pressed against the jump of a faltering pulse. The web of filaments he had torn from Thorne’s skull now lay in a brittle nest around them, each thread a severed umbilical. The link was broken. What was left in the vessel was only what had been poured in before the spigot was sealed: fear, and fragments.

“Can you stand?” Pieter’s voice was a rasp, stripped of all resonance by the consuming dark.

The answer was a choked gasp. Pieter shifted, getting his shoulder under Thorne’s arm, and heaved. They rose together, unsteady as sailors on a tilting deck. The floor trembled with a new, rhythmic shudder. Boom. Boom. Not an explosion. Something heavier. The footfalls of a dying giant. The spire’s structural integrity was unspooling, floor by floor.

He had no plan, only a vector: away from the heart-room, away from the source of the cascading scream. The corridor was a black throat. He remembered the door, now sealed shut by the quarantine protocols his virus had temporarily bypassed. It would not open again. He began to move, dragging Thorne with him, one shuffling step at a time, his free hand trailing along the wall. The concrete was no longer smooth. He felt cracks, hairline at first, then widening to gaps he could slip his fingers into. Dust poured from them in constant, whispering streams.

A memory surfaced, unbidden: the portolan chart on his desk in Ghent. The vellum, genuine, salvaged. The forger’s first rule: know your substrate. Understand its weaknesses, its history of stress. This spire was his substrate now. It had a history. It had been built upon the old city, the tangled, rebellious mess of it. They had not erased that foundation; they had capped it, buried it under flawless geometry. But the fault lines remained. His virus, his act of precise, artistic vandalism, had simply found them. It had not brought the spire down. It had reminded the spire what lay beneath.

The wall fell away. An intersection. The darkness was less absolute here, stained a faint, sickly green by emergency bioluminescent strips running along the floor—a backup system, ancient and biological, now guttering to life. The light was just enough to see the carnage. The corridor to the left was buckled, the ceiling slumped into a jagged maw of reinforcing rods and shattered polymer. From within came a sound of rushing water, a main severed. The right-hand passage was clear, but the green strips led towards a downward-sloping ramp.

Down. Away from the screaming. Towards the foundations.

Thorne’s weight increased, his steps more faltering. “The… numbers,” he whispered, his voice the scrape of two dry stones. “They’re… falling.”

“What numbers?”

“All of them.” Thorne’s head lolled. “The consensus. The edit. It’s… reverting. The original data… it’s bleeding through.”

Pieter adjusted his grip, his mind working. The original data. The unedited truth. The chaotic, contradictory, human record of what had happened here, before the spire’s clean narrative was imposed. It was returning. Not as a file, but as a structural flaw. The tapestry was unravelling, and the picture emerging from the loose threads was the one they had tried to cut away.

They stumbled down the ramp. The green light pulsed, organic and slow. The air grew colder, damper. The symphony of destruction above was muted here, replaced by a low, tectonic grumble. The ramp opened into a vast, cylindrical space—a utility conduit, lined with pulsating fibre-optic bundles and thick, insulated pipes. It was a forgotten artery. And it was not empty.

Figures moved in the gloom. Not black-uniformed officers. These were clad in drab maintenance coveralls, their faces pale in the bioluminescent glow. They worked with a frantic, silent urgency, hauling equipment crates, sealing bulkhead doors. One saw Pieter and Thorne and froze, a hand going to the tool at his belt. Pieter met his gaze, saw not alarm, but a desperate calculation.

“The heart-room is breached,” Pieter said, his voice carrying in the damp air. “The cascade has passed the point of containment.”

The man—his face was young, but etched with a fatigue that had nothing to do with physical labour—nodded once, sharply. “We know. The fallback is compromised. The deep archive is flooding.”

“Flooding?”

“With the old data. The raw feed. It’s… volatile. It’s overloading the physical buffers.” He gestured down the conduit. “The lower silos are melting down.”

Another worker jogged up, a woman with her hair tied back in a severe knot. “Jansen, we need to seal this junction. Now.” Her eyes flicked to Pieter, assessing. “Who are you? Your neural signature is… offline.”

“I’m the solvent,” Pieter said, the truth leaving him with a strange relief. “I’m the flaw. And he…” He shifted Thorne’s weight. “He is a witness. The first witness.”

The woman, Jansen, exchanged a look with her colleague. A silent communication passed between them. The corruption of power had not spared these people; it had merely assigned them to its plumbing. They were the ones who handled the waste, who knew where the bodies, and the truths, were buried. They understood the value of a witness, and the greater value of a flaw that had broken the system that enslaved them.

“This way,” Jansen said, turning. “The deep archive. If you want to see the edit… before it dissolves.”

They did not help him with Thorne. This was his burden, his proof. He followed them, half-carrying, half-dragging the broken analyst. The conduit narrowed, then terminated at a heavy, circular door like a bank vault’s, its mechanism manual, a great wheel at its centre. Two other workers were straining against it. With a shriek of protesting metal, it gave way, swinging inward.

The air that washed out was different. Not the sterile, processed chill of the spire, nor the damp mildew of the conduit. It was dry, and carried a scent Pieter knew intimately from a lifetime spent with fragile things: the smell of slow decay. Of paper, ink, and dust.

Jansen grabbed one of the wall-mounted glow-strips, wrenching it free. Its soft green light pushed into the space beyond.

It was a library. But not of books. The chamber was vast, disappearing into darkness in every direction. Stacked in endless, towering rows were data-silos—crystalline obelisks, each the size of a man, glowing with a faint internal light. But the light was sickly, flickering. And between the silos, spilling from access ports, pooling on the floor, was a substance like liquid mercury, but shot through with pulses of colour and static. It was the raw, unedited data-stream, the consensus reality before it was processed, sanitised, and made seamless. It was bleeding from its containers, a toxic, truthful spill.

“The original record,” Jansen said, her voice hushed. “Every sensor feed, every citizen’s neural log, every public and private communication from the Unification Period. The ‘before.’ They couldn’t delete it. The paradox of total edit: you must keep the original to prove the improvement. So they buried it. Here. In the spire’s shadow.”

Pieter led Thorne to a clear spot near the door, propping him against a silo whose surface was webbed with cracks, the liquid data seeping out like silvery blood. Thorne’s eyes were open, fixed on the leaking light. He was trembling.

“It’s all here?” Pieter asked.

“All of it. The contradictions. The protests. The… accidents.” Jansen’s face was grim. “The Mass Calibration at the Grand Plaza. The one they edited into a peaceful transition.”

Pieter walked forward, his boots careful on the floor, avoiding the pools of shimmering data. He approached the nearest cracked silo. He reached out, not touching, but letting his hand hover near the leak. A cascade of images, sounds, emotions—not a narrative, but a cacophony—pressed against his mind. A shouted slogan. The smell of tear gas. The cold weight of a rifle stock. A child crying. A shot, ringing out, then another, then a terrible, rolling thunder of them. It was not a memory he owned, but it was a memory that existed, here, in this cold tomb. It was the loose thread.

The corruption was complete. The power that built this place had corrupted itself by believing it could sculpt truth like clay. And his own power, the forger’s power to unmake, had corrupted him into an instrument of absolute negation. He saw now that the two corruptions were a closed loop. The act of perfect control bred the perfect flaw. The masterpiece demanded the critic who would, eventually, apply the solvent.

A deep, resonant clang echoed through the archive, followed by a shudder that made the towers of silos sway. A fine mist of crystalline dust sifted down from the unseen ceiling.

“The structural cascade has reached the sub-levels,” the young man said, his voice tight. “This chamber won’t hold.”

“We need to extract the core witness logs,” Jansen said, moving with renewed desperation towards a central console, its screens a chaos of error sigils. “If the archive goes, the proof goes with it.”

“Proof for whom?” Pieter asked, still watching the silver seep. “There is no outside authority. The spire is the authority.”

“For after,” she said, her fingers flying over the dead console. “For whatever comes after the spire.”

Thorne let out a low moan. Pieter turned. The man had pressed his palm flat against the cracked surface of the silo beside him. The liquid data was flowing over his skin, up his arm, drawn like iron filings to a magnet. His eyes were wide, blind, reflecting the chaotic pulse of the leak.

“It’s… singing,” Thorne whispered. “All the voices. They’re all singing at once. They’re saying… they’re saying no.”

The edit was not just reverting. It was speaking. The buried truth was using the broken vessel of Elias Thorne as its mouthpiece.

Pieter crossed to him, knelt. He did not pull Thorne’s hand away. This, too, was a form of witnessing. “What else do they say?”

Thorne’s head twitched. A string of saliva hung from his lip. “They say… it was a choice. Not a calibration. A choice. To look away. To say the edit was peace.” His voice gained a terrible, choral quality, as if layered with a thousand whispers. “The guns were in the square. The order was given. The edit was applied… after. To the living, not the dead. They made us forget we saw. They made us agree… to the version where we didn’t look.”

The corruption was not just in the act, but in the memory of the act. It was in the willing, edited consensus. The power had been so total it had rewritten not only history, but the conscience of those who had lived it.

Another shudder, stronger. With a sound like a mountain sighing, a data-silo three rows over listed, cracked at its base, and toppled. It fell in slow motion, shearing through its neighbours before hitting the floor in an explosion of crystalline shards and a tidal wave of liquid silver data. The wave surged towards them, a glittering, mind-drowning flood.

Jansen screamed a warning. Pieter acted on an instinct older than reason. He threw himself over Thorne, not to shield him from debris, but from the data. The physical impact was nothing. The psychic one was an avalanche.

It was not a single narrative. It was the opposite. It was the Grand Plaza from ten thousand angles at once—the fear of the soldier fingering his trigger, the fury of the protester throwing a stone, the confusion of the child pulled by her mother, the cold calculus of the official in a safe room watching a feed, the visceral shock of the first body hitting the cobbles, the second, the twentieth. It was the stunning, unbearable noise of happening. And then, like a film reel spliced, it was the silence. The clean-up. The official statements. The gentle, neural pressure to accept, to agree, to find the peace in the new, edited memory. To be grateful for the seamless truth.

The wave passed. Pieter lay gasping, his skull a bell that had been struck with a hammer. Beneath him, Thorne was silent, still. The data had receded, leaving them soaked in its residue, their clothes glittering faintly. Pieter pushed himself up. His senses were raw, overloaded. He could still hear the echoes of the gunshots, the real ones, the ones that had been edited into the memory of a backfire, a celebratory firecracker.

Jansen was helping her colleague up. The archive was dying around them. Silo after silo was darkening, cracking, vomiting their contents. The sea of silver was rising.

“We have to go!” the young man yelled over the rising din of structural agony.

Pieter looked at Thorne. The man’s eyes were closed. His breathing was shallow, but even. The chorus had left him. He was just a man again, empty of everything, even the edited lies.

“Go where?” Pieter asked, his voice calm in the centre of the storm. “The spire is falling. The edit is unspooling. There is no ‘after’ for this. Only the during.”

He understood his role now. He was not a saviour. He was not a liberator. He was the flaw that had found the fault line. His corruption was to be the one who would not agree, who would not hold the secular chalice of consensus. His final forgery would be the absence of a forgery. He would bear witness to the unmaking.

He got to his feet, hauling Thorne up once more. “The way you came in. Is it still passable?”

Jansen nodded, her face etched with terror and a wild hope. “The old service shafts. They lead to the foundations. To the old city.”

The original substrate. The vellum beneath the masterpiece.

“Lead us,” Pieter said.

They fled through the dying archive, a small, desperate procession in the guttering green light. The conduit behind the vault door was now shuddering violently, pipes bursting, spraying steam and coolant. They ran, past junctions where the darkness was now split with the violent orange glare of electrical fires, past alcoves where maintenance workers huddled, their certainties evaporating with the light.

Jansen led them to a grate, rusted and half-hidden behind a fallen bundle of cables. She and the young man wrestled it open. A yawning, vertical shaft greeted them, smelling of damp earth and antiquity. A fixed ladder, iron and pitted, vanished into the black below.

“It goes all the way down,” Jansen said. “To the old storm drains. They connect to the river.”

Pieter looked at Thorne, then at the shaft. It was a descent into the wound. He nodded. He went first, guiding Thorne’s limp feet onto the rungs above him, bearing the man’s weight as they climbed down into the absolute dark, leaving the green glow behind. The others followed.

The climb was an eternity in a cold, tight throat. The sounds of the spire’s death throes faded, replaced by the drip of water, the scuttle of unseen things, the deep, patient silence of earth. The air grew thick and cold. The rungs ended in a shallow, flowing stream of black water. They were in a brick tunnel, arched and ancient, part of the city that had been sealed away, forgotten, buried alive by the gleaming spire above.

Jansen activated a small hand-torch. Its beam cut through the gloom, illuminating dripping walls covered in strange, faded graffiti—not the sleek sigils of the spire, but the angry, urgent scrawls of the world before. Names. Dates. Cries of defiance that had been silenced by concrete and consensus.

They waded through the icy water, directionless. The tunnel branched, and branched again. A labyrinth of the rejected past.
